<p><b>James Abram Garfield</b> (November 19, 1831&#160;– September 19, 1881) was the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="List of Presidents of the United States">20th</a> <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President of the United States</a>, serving from March 4, 1881 until <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_James_A._Garfield" title="Assassination of James A. Garfield">his assassination</a> later that year. Garfield had served nine terms in the House of Representatives, and had been elected to the Senate before his candidacy for the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a>, though he declined the senatorship once he was <a href="/wiki/President-elect_of_the_United_States" title="President-elect of the United States">president-elect</a>. He is the only sitting House member to be elected president.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Garfield was raised in humble circumstances on an Ohio farm by his widowed mother. He worked at various jobs, including on a <a href="/wiki/Barge" title="Barge">canal boat</a>, in his youth. Beginning at age 17, he attended several Ohio schools, then studied at <a href="/wiki/Williams_College" title="Williams College">Williams College</a> in <a href="/wiki/Williamstown,_Massachusetts" title="Williamstown, Massachusetts">Williamstown, Massachusetts</a>, from which he graduated in 1856. A year later, Garfield entered politics as a <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican</a>. He married <a href="/wiki/Lucretia_Garfield" title="Lucretia Garfield">Lucretia Rudolph</a> in 1858, and served as a member of the <a href="/wiki/Ohio_Senate" title="Ohio Senate">Ohio State Senate</a> (1859–1861). Garfield opposed <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederate secession</a>, served as a major general in the <a href="/wiki/Union_Army" title="Union Army">Union Army</a> during the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>, and fought in the battles of <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Middle_Creek" title="Battle of Middle Creek">Middle Creek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Shiloh" title="Battle of Shiloh">Shiloh</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Chickamauga" title="Battle of Chickamauga">Chickamauga</a>. He was first elected to Congress in 1862 to represent <a href="/wiki/Ohio%27s_19th_congressional_district" title="Ohio's 19th congressional district">Ohio's 19th District</a>. Throughout Garfield's extended congressional service after the Civil War, he firmly supported the <a href="/wiki/Gold_standard" title="Gold standard">gold standard</a> and gained a reputation as a skilled orator. Garfield initially agreed with <a href="/wiki/Radical_Republicans" title="Radical Republicans" class="mw-redirect">Radical Republican</a> views regarding <a href="/wiki/Reconstruction_Era_of_the_United_States" title="Reconstruction Era of the United States" class="mw-redirect">Reconstruction</a>, but later favored a moderate approach for civil rights enforcement for <a href="/wiki/Freedman" title="Freedman">freedmen</a>.</p>
<p>At the <a href="/wiki/1880_Republican_National_Convention" title="1880 Republican National Convention">1880 Republican National Convention</a>, Senator-elect Garfield attended as campaign manager for <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Treasury" title="United States Secretary of the Treasury">Secretary of the Treasury</a> <a href="/wiki/John_Sherman" title="John Sherman">John Sherman</a>, and gave the presidential nomination speech for him. When neither Sherman nor his rivals&#160;– <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant" title="Ulysses S. Grant">Ulysses S. Grant</a> and <a href="/wiki/James_G._Blaine" title="James G. Blaine">James G. Blaine</a>&#160;– could get enough votes to secure the nomination, delegates chose Garfield as a compromise on the 36th ballot. In the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1880" title="United States presidential election, 1880">1880 presidential election</a>, Garfield conducted a low-key <a href="/wiki/Front_porch_campaign" title="Front porch campaign">front porch campaign</a>, and narrowly defeated Democrat <a href="/wiki/Winfield_Scott_Hancock" title="Winfield Scott Hancock">Winfield Scott Hancock</a>.</p>
<p>Garfield's accomplishments as president included a resurgence of presidential authority against <a href="/wiki/Senatorial_courtesy" title="Senatorial courtesy">senatorial courtesy</a> in executive appointments, energizing <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">American naval</a> power, and purging corruption in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Post_Office_Department" title="United States Post Office Department">Post Office</a>, all during his extremely short time in office. Garfield made notable diplomatic and judiciary appointments, including a <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">U.S. Supreme Court</a> justice. He enhanced the powers of the presidency when he defied the powerful New York senator <a href="/wiki/Roscoe_Conkling" title="Roscoe Conkling">Roscoe Conkling</a> by appointing <a href="/wiki/William_H._Robertson" title="William H. Robertson">William H. Robertson</a> to the lucrative post of <a href="/wiki/Collector_of_the_Port_of_New_York" title="Collector of the Port of New York">Collector of the Port of New York</a>, starting a fracas that ended with Robertson's confirmation and Conkling's resignation from the Senate. Garfield advocated agricultural technology, an educated electorate, and civil rights for <a href="/wiki/African-American" title="African-American" class="mw-redirect">African-Americans</a>. He also proposed substantial civil service reform, eventually passed by Congress in 1883 and signed into law by his successor, <a href="/wiki/Chester_A._Arthur" title="Chester A. Arthur">Chester A. Arthur</a>, as the <a href="/wiki/Pendleton_Civil_Service_Reform_Act" title="Pendleton Civil Service Reform Act">Pendleton Civil Service Reform Act</a>. With his term cut short by his death after only 200 days, and much of it spent in ill health trying to recover from the attack, Garfield is little-remembered in the American cultural memory other than for his assassination; historians often forego <a href="/wiki/Historical_rankings_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="Historical rankings of Presidents of the United States">listing him in rankings of U.S. presidents</a> due to the short length of his presidency.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Childhood">Childhood</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_A._Garfield&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Childhood">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:MorelandHillsGarfieldCabin.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A log cabin with a statue and a tree in front" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/MorelandHillsGarfieldCabin.jpg/220px-MorelandHillsGarfieldCabin.jpg" width="220" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/MorelandHillsGarfieldCabin.jpg/330px-MorelandHillsGarfieldCabin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/MorelandHillsGarfieldCabin.jpg/440px-MorelandHillsGarfieldCabin.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="1024" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:MorelandHillsGarfieldCabin.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Birthplace site of James Garfield</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>James Garfield was born the youngest of five children on November 19, 1831, in a log cabin in <a href="/wiki/Orange_Township,_Cuyahoga_County,_Ohio" title="Orange Township, Cuyahoga County, Ohio" class="mw-redirect">Orange Township</a>, now <a href="/wiki/Moreland_Hills,_Ohio" title="Moreland Hills, Ohio">Moreland Hills, Ohio</a>. Orange Township was located in the <a href="/wiki/Western_Reserve" title="Western Reserve" class="mw-redirect">Western Reserve</a>, and like many who settled there, Garfield's ancestors were from New England. James' father Abram had been born in <a href="/wiki/Worcester,_New_York" title="Worcester, New York">Worcester, New York</a> and came to Ohio to woo his childhood sweetheart, Mehitabel Ballou, only to find her married. He instead wed her sister Eliza, who had been born in New Hampshire. James was named for an older brother, dead in infancy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin19784.E2.80.936_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin19784.E2.80.936-4"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In early 1833, Abram and Eliza Garfield joined the <a href="/wiki/Disciples_of_Christ" title="Disciples of Christ" class="mw-redirect">Disciples of Christ</a>, a decision that would help shape their youngest son's life.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin19786.E2.80.937_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin19786.E2.80.937-5"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup> Abram Garfield died later that year; his son was raised in poverty in a household led by the strong-willed Eliza.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin19788.E2.80.9310_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin19788.E2.80.9310-6"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> James was her favorite child, and the two remained close for the rest of her life.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERutkow20064_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERutkow20064-7"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> Eliza Garfield remarried in 1842, but soon left her second husband, Warren Belden (possibly Alfred Belden), and a then-scandalous divorce was awarded against her in 1850. James took his mother's side, and when Belden died in 1880 noted the fact in his diary with satisfaction.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin197810.E2.80.9311_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin197810.E2.80.9311-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> Garfield enjoyed his mother's stories about his ancestry, especially his <a href="/wiki/Welsh_people" title="Welsh people">Welsh</a> great-great-grandfathers and his ancestor who served as a knight of <a href="/wiki/Caerphilly_Castle" title="Caerphilly Castle">Caerffili Castle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown188123_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown188123-9"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Poor and fatherless, Garfield was mocked by his fellow boys, and throughout his life was very sensitive to slights. He escaped through reading, devouring all the books he could find.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin197810.E2.80.9311_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin197810.E2.80.9311-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> He left home at age 16 in 1847. Rejected by the only ship in port in <a href="/wiki/Cleveland,_Ohio" title="Cleveland, Ohio" class="mw-redirect">Cleveland</a>, Garfield instead found work on a canal boat, responsible for managing the mules that pulled it.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown188130.E2.80.9333_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown188130.E2.80.9333-10"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> This labor would be used to good effect by <a href="/wiki/Horatio_Alger" title="Horatio Alger" class="mw-redirect">Horatio Alger</a>, who penned Garfield's campaign biography in 1880.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERutkow200610_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERutkow200610-11"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After six weeks, illness forced Garfield to return home and, during his recuperation, his mother and a local education official got him to promise to postpone his return to the canals for a year and go to school. Accordingly, in 1848, he began at Geauga Seminary, in nearby <a href="/wiki/Chester_Township,_Geauga_County,_Ohio" title="Chester Township, Geauga County, Ohio">Chester Township</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin197814.E2.80.9317_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin197814.E2.80.9317-12"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> Garfield later said of his childhood, "I lament that I was born to poverty, and in this chaos of childhood, seventeen years passed before I caught any inspiration&#160;... a precious 17 years when a boy with a father and some wealth might have become fixed in manly ways."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin197813_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin197813-13"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Education.2C_marriage_and_early_career">Education, marriage and early career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_A._Garfield&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Education, marriage and early career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Garfield-at-16.jpg" class="image"><img alt="An unsmiling young man with curly hair wearing a three piece suit" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Garfield-at-16.jpg/170px-Garfield-at-16.jpg" width="170" height="232" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Garfield-at-16.jpg/255px-Garfield-at-16.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/91/Garfield-at-16.jpg 2x" data-file-width="257" data-file-height="350" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Garfield-at-16.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Garfield at age 16</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At Geauga Academy, which he attended from 1848 to 1850, Garfield learned academic subjects he had not previously had time for. He shone as a student, and was especially interested in languages and elocution. He began to appreciate the power a speaker had over an audience, writing that the speaker's platform "creates some excitement. I love agitation and investigation and glory in defending unpopular truth against popular error."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERutkow20066_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERutkow20066-14"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> Geauga was co-educational, and Garfield was attracted to one of his fellow students, <a href="/wiki/Lucretia_Garfield" title="Lucretia Garfield">Lucretia Rudolph</a>, whom he later married.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown188171.E2.80.9373_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown188171.E2.80.9373-15"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> To support himself at Geauga, he worked as a carpenter's assistant and as a teacher.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown188147.E2.80.9349_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown188147.E2.80.9349-16"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> The need to go from town to town to find a place as a teacher disgusted Garfield, and he thereafter developed a dislike of what he called "place-seeking", which became, he said, "the law of my life".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin197816_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin197816-17"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> In later years, he would astound his friends by letting positions pass that could have been his with a little politicking.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin197816_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin197816-17"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> Garfield had attended church more to please his mother than to worship God, but in his late teens underwent a religious awakening, and attended many <a href="/wiki/Camp_meeting" title="Camp meeting">camp meetings</a>, at one of which he was <a href="/wiki/Born_again_(Christianity)" title="Born again (Christianity)">born again</a>. The next day, March 4, 1850, he was baptized into the Disciples by being submerged in the icy waters of the <a href="/wiki/Chagrin_River" title="Chagrin River">Chagrin River</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin197817_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin197817-18"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lucretia_Garfield_-_Brady-Handy.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Lucretia_Garfield_-_Brady-Handy.jpg/220px-Lucretia_Garfield_-_Brady-Handy.jpg" width="220" height="292" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Lucretia_Garfield_-_Brady-Handy.jpg/330px-Lucretia_Garfield_-_Brady-Handy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Lucretia_Garfield_-_Brady-Handy.jpg/440px-Lucretia_Garfield_-_Brady-Handy.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3024" data-file-height="4016" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lucretia_Garfield_-_Brady-Handy.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Lucretia_Garfield" title="Lucretia Garfield">Lucretia Garfield</a> in the 1870s</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After leaving Geauga, Garfield worked for a year at various jobs, including teaching.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin197821_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin197821-19"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup> Finding that some New Englanders worked their way through college, Garfield determined to do the same, and first sought a school that could prepare him for the entrance examinations. From 1851 to 1854, he attended the Western Reserve Eclectic Institute (later named <a href="/wiki/Hiram_College" title="Hiram College">Hiram College</a>) in <a href="/wiki/Hiram,_Ohio" title="Hiram, Ohio">Hiram, Ohio</a>, a school run by the Disciples. While there, he was most interested in the study of Greek and Latin, but was inclined to learn about and discuss any new thing he encountered.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin197827.E2.80.9328_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin197827.E2.80.9328-20"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> Securing a position on entry as <a href="/wiki/Janitor" title="Janitor">janitor</a>, he was hired to teach while still a student.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin197822.E2.80.9323_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin197822.E2.80.9323-21"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> Lucretia Rudolph had also enrolled at the Institute, and Garfield wooed her while teaching her Greek.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin197829_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin197829-22"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> He developed a regular preaching circuit at neighboring churches, in some cases earning a <a href="/wiki/Gold_dollar" title="Gold dollar">gold dollar</a> per service. By 1854, Garfield had learned all the Institute could teach him and was a full-time teacher.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown188156Peskin197830_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown188156Peskin197830-23"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup> Garfield then enrolled at <a href="/wiki/Williams_College" title="Williams College">Williams College</a> in <a href="/wiki/Williamstown,_Massachusetts" title="Williamstown, Massachusetts">Williamstown, Massachusetts</a>, as a <a href="/wiki/Junior_(education)" title="Junior (education)">third-year student</a>, given credit for two year's study at the Institute after passing a cursory examination. Garfield was impressed with the college president, <a href="/wiki/Mark_Hopkins_(educator)" title="Mark Hopkins (educator)">Mark Hopkins</a>, who had responded warmly to Garfield's letter inquiring about admission. He said of Hopkins, "The ideal college is Mark Hopkins on one end of a log with a student on the other."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin197834_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin197834-24"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> Hopkins later stated about Garfield in his student days, "There was a large general capacity applicable to any subject. There was no pretense of genius, or alternation of spasmodic effort, but a satisfactory accomplishment in all directions."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERutkow20068_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERutkow20068-25"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup> After his first term, Garfield was hired to teach penmanship to the students of nearby <a href="/wiki/Pownal,_Vermont" title="Pownal, Vermont">Pownal, Vermont</a>—a post whose previous incumbent was <a href="/wiki/Chester_A._Arthur" title="Chester A. Arthur">Chester A. Arthur</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERutkow20068_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERutkow20068-25"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Garfield graduated from Williams in August 1856 as <a href="/wiki/Salutatorian" title="Salutatorian">salutatorian</a>, giving an address at the <a href="/wiki/Graduation" title="Graduation">commencement</a>. Garfield biographer Ira Rutkow pointed out that the future president's years at Williams gave Garfield the opportunity to know and respect those of different social backgrounds, and despite his origin as an unsophisticated Westerner, he was liked and respected by socially conscious New Englanders. "In short," as Rutkow later wrote, "Garfield had an extensive and positive first experience with the world outside the Western Reserve of Ohio."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERutkow20068_25-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERutkow20068-25"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On his return to Ohio, the degree from a prestigious Eastern school made Garfield a man of distinction. He returned to Hiram to teach at the Institute, and in 1857 was made its president. He did not see education as a field in which he could realize his full potential. At Williams, he had become more politically aware in the intensely anti-slavery atmosphere of the Massachusetts school, and began to consider politics as a career.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERutkow200611_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERutkow200611-26"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1858, he married Lucretia; they would have seven children, five of whom survived infancy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERutkow200644_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERutkow200644-27"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup> Soon after the wedding, he formally entered his name to <a href="/wiki/Read_law" title="Read law" class="mw-redirect">read law</a> at a Cleveland firm, although he did his studying in Hiram.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown188174.E2.80.9375_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown188174.E2.80.9375-28"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> He was admitted to the bar in 1861.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin197882_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin197882-29"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Local <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican Party</a> leaders invited Garfield to enter politics upon the death of Cyrus Prentiss, the presumptive nominee for the local state senate seat. He was nominated by the party convention on the sixth ballot, and was elected, serving until 1861.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin197860.E2.80.9361_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin197860.E2.80.9361-30"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> Garfield's major effort in the state senate was a bill providing for Ohio's first <a href="/wiki/Geological_survey" title="Geological survey">geological survey</a> to measure its mineral resources, though it failed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin197873_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin197873-31"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_War">Civil War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_A._Garfield&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Civil War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:General_James_Garfield_-_Brady-Handy.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Seated portrait in army uniform. Garfield has a full beard and mustache" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/General_James_Garfield_-_Brady-Handy.jpg/170px-General_James_Garfield_-_Brady-Handy.jpg" width="170" height="243" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/General_James_Garfield_-_Brady-Handy.jpg/255px-General_James_Garfield_-_Brady-Handy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/General_James_Garfield_-_Brady-Handy.jpg/340px-General_James_Garfield_-_Brady-Handy.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2936" data-file-height="4192" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:General_James_Garfield_-_Brady-Handy.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Garfield as a brigadier general during the Civil War</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a>'s election as president, several Southern states announced their <a href="/wiki/Secession_in_the_United_States" title="Secession in the United States">secession</a> from the Union to form a new government, the <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederate States of America</a>. Garfield read military texts while anxiously awaiting the war effort, which he regarded as a holy crusade against the <a href="/wiki/Slave_Power" title="Slave Power">Slave Power</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin197886.E2.80.9387_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin197886.E2.80.9387-32"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> In April 1861, the rebels <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Fort_Sumter" title="Battle of Fort Sumter">bombarded Fort Sumter</a>, one of the last federal outposts in the South, beginning the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">Civil War</a>. Although he had no military training, Garfield knew that his place was in the Union Army.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin197886.E2.80.9387_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin197886.E2.80.9387-32"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>At Governor <a href="/wiki/William_Dennison_Jr." title="William Dennison Jr.">William Dennison's</a> request, Garfield deferred his military ambitions to remain in the legislature, where he helped appropriate the funds to raise and equip Ohio's volunteer regiments.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin197887.E2.80.9389_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin197887.E2.80.9389-33"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> Afterward, the legislature adjourned and Garfield spent the spring and early summer on a speaking tour of northeastern Ohio, encouraging enlistment in the new regiments.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin197887.E2.80.9389_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin197887.E2.80.9389-33"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> Following a trip to Illinois to purchase muskets, Garfield returned to Ohio and, in August 1861, received a commission as a <a href="/wiki/Colonel_(United_States)" title="Colonel (United States)">colonel</a> in the <a href="/wiki/42nd_Ohio_Infantry" title="42nd Ohio Infantry">42nd Ohio Infantry</a> regiment.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin197890.E2.80.9393_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin197890.E2.80.9393-34"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> The 42nd Ohio existed only on paper, so Garfield's first task was to fill its ranks. He did so quickly, recruiting many of his neighbors and former students.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin197890.E2.80.9393_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin197890.E2.80.9393-34"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> The regiment traveled to <a href="/wiki/Camp_Chase" title="Camp Chase">Camp Chase</a>, outside <a href="/wiki/Columbus,_Ohio" title="Columbus, Ohio">Columbus, Ohio</a>, to complete training.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin197890.E2.80.9393_34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin197890.E2.80.9393-34"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> In December, Garfield was ordered to bring the 42nd to Kentucky, where they joined the <a href="/wiki/Army_of_the_Ohio" title="Army of the Ohio">Army of the Ohio</a> under <a href="/wiki/Brigadier_general_(United_States)" title="Brigadier general (United States)">Brigadier General</a> <a href="/wiki/Don_Carlos_Buell" title="Don Carlos Buell">Don Carlos Buell</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin197898.E2.80.93101_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin197898.E2.80.93101-35"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Buell.27s_command">Buell's command</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_A._Garfield&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Buell's command">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Buell quickly assigned Garfield the task of driving Confederate forces out of eastern Kentucky, giving him the 18th Brigade for the campaign which, besides his own 42nd, included the <a href="/wiki/40th_Ohio_Infantry" title="40th Ohio Infantry">40th Ohio Infantry</a>, two Kentucky infantry regiments and two cavalry units.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978101.E2.80.93103_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978101.E2.80.93103-36"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> They departed <a href="/wiki/Catlettsburg,_Kentucky" title="Catlettsburg, Kentucky">Catlettsburg, Kentucky</a>, in mid-December, advancing through the valley of the <a href="/wiki/Big_Sandy_River_(Ohio_River)" title="Big Sandy River (Ohio River)">Big Sandy River</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978101.E2.80.93103_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978101.E2.80.93103-36"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> The march was uneventful until Union forces reached <a href="/wiki/Paintsville,_Kentucky" title="Paintsville, Kentucky">Paintsville, Kentucky</a>, on January 6, 1862, where Garfield's cavalry engaged the rebels at Jenny's Creek.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978106.E2.80.93112_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978106.E2.80.93112-37"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> Confederate troops under Brigadier General <a href="/wiki/Humphrey_Marshall_(general)" title="Humphrey Marshall (general)">Humphrey Marshall</a> held the town in numbers roughly equal to Garfield's own, but Garfield positioned his troops so as to deceive Marshall into believing that rebel forces were outnumbered.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978106.E2.80.93112_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978106.E2.80.93112-37"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> Marshall ordered his troops to withdraw to the forks of Middle Creek, on the road to Virginia; Garfield ordered his troops to pursue the Confederates.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978112.E2.80.93115_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978112.E2.80.93115-38"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> They attacked the rebel positions on January 9, 1862, in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Middle_Creek" title="Battle of Middle Creek">Battle of Middle Creek</a>, the only pitched battle Garfield personally commanded.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaldwell196576.E2.80.9378_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaldwell196576.E2.80.9378-39"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> At the end of the fighting, the Confederates withdrew from the field, and Garfield sent his troops to <a href="/wiki/Prestonsburg,_Kentucky" title="Prestonsburg, Kentucky">Prestonsburg</a> to reprovision.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978116.E2.80.93120_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978116.E2.80.93120-40"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Middle_Creek_Battlefield.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Middle_Creek_Battlefield.jpg/250px-Middle_Creek_Battlefield.jpg" width="250" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Middle_Creek_Battlefield.jpg/375px-Middle_Creek_Battlefield.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Middle_Creek_Battlefield.jpg/500px-Middle_Creek_Battlefield.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6016" data-file-height="4000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Middle_Creek_Battlefield.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Middle Creek battlefield; Garfield commanded from the distant hill in the center of the photo</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In recognition of his success, Garfield was promoted to brigadier general.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978128_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978128-41"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> After Marshall's retreat, Garfield's command was the sole remaining Union force in eastern Kentucky, and he announced that any men who had fought for the Confederacy would be granted amnesty if they returned to their homes and lived peaceably and remained loyal to the Union.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978122.E2.80.93127_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978122.E2.80.93127-42"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> The proclamation was surprisingly lenient, as Garfield now believed the war was a crusade for eradication of slavery.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978122.E2.80.93127_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978122.E2.80.93127-42"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> Following a brief skirmish at <a href="/wiki/Pound_Gap" title="Pound Gap">Pound Gap</a>, the last rebel units in the area were outflanked, and they retreated to Virginia.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaldwell196581.E2.80.9382_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaldwell196581.E2.80.9382-43"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Garfield's promotion gave him command of the 20th Brigade of the Army of the Ohio, which was ordered in early 1862 to join <a href="/wiki/Major_general_(United_States)" title="Major general (United States)">Major General</a> <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant" title="Ulysses S. Grant">Ulysses S. Grant</a>'s forces as they advanced on <a href="/wiki/Corinth,_Mississippi" title="Corinth, Mississippi">Corinth, Mississippi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978131.E2.80.93133_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978131.E2.80.93133-44"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> Before the 20th Brigade arrived, however, Confederate forces under General <a href="/wiki/Albert_Sidney_Johnston" title="Albert Sidney Johnston">Albert Sidney Johnston</a> surprised Grant's men in their camps, driving them back.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978134.E2.80.93135_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978134.E2.80.93135-45"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> Garfield's troops got word of the battle and advanced quickly, joining the rest of the army on the second day to drive the Confederates back across the field and into retreat.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978135.E2.80.93137_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978135.E2.80.93137-46"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup> The action, later known as the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Shiloh" title="Battle of Shiloh">Battle of Shiloh</a>, was the bloodiest of the war to date; Garfield was exposed to fire for much of the day, but emerged uninjured.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978135.E2.80.93137_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978135.E2.80.93137-46"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup> Major General <a href="/wiki/Henry_W._Halleck" title="Henry W. Halleck" class="mw-redirect">Henry W. Halleck</a>, Grant's superior, took charge of the combined armies and advanced ponderously toward Corinth; when they arrived, the Confederates had fled.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978138.E2.80.93139_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978138.E2.80.93139-47"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>That summer Garfield suffered from <a href="/wiki/Jaundice" title="Jaundice">jaundice</a> and significant weight loss.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span>[</span>b<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978146.E2.80.93147_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978146.E2.80.93147-50"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> He was forced to return home, where his wife nursed him back to health.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978146.E2.80.93147_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978146.E2.80.93147-50"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> While he was home, Garfield's friends worked to gain him the Republican nomination for Congress, although he refused to politick with the delegates.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978147.E2.80.93148_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978147.E2.80.93148-51"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> He returned to duty that autumn and went to Washington to await his next assignment.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978149.E2.80.93151_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978149.E2.80.93151-52"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> During this period of idleness, a rumor of an extra-marital affair caused friction in the Garfield marriage until Lucretia eventually chose to overlook it.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978160.E2.80.93161_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978160.E2.80.93161-53"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup> Garfield repeatedly received tentative assignments that were quickly withdrawn, to his frustration.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978161.E2.80.93162_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978161.E2.80.93162-54"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup> In the meantime, he served on the <a href="/wiki/Court-martial_of_Fitz_John_Porter" title="Court-martial of Fitz John Porter">court-martial of Fitz John Porter</a>, a sensational trial in which <a href="/wiki/Fitz_John_Porter" title="Fitz John Porter">Porter</a> was tried for his tardiness at the <a href="/wiki/Second_Battle_of_Bull_Run" title="Second Battle of Bull Run">Second Battle of Bull Run</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978162.E2.80.93165_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978162.E2.80.93165-55"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> He was convinced of Porter's guilt, and voted with his fellow generals to convict.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978162.E2.80.93165_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978162.E2.80.93165-55"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> The trial lasted almost two months, from November 1862 to January 1863, and by the end of it, Garfield had at last procured an assignment as Chief of Staff to Major General <a href="/wiki/William_S._Rosecrans" title="William S. Rosecrans" class="mw-redirect">William S. Rosecrans</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978166_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978166-56"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chief_of_staff_for_Rosecrans">Chief of staff for Rosecrans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_A._Garfield&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Chief of staff for Rosecrans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:GenWmSRosecrans.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/GenWmSRosecrans.jpg/170px-GenWmSRosecrans.jpg" width="170" height="241" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/GenWmSRosecrans.jpg/255px-GenWmSRosecrans.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/GenWmSRosecrans.jpg/340px-GenWmSRosecrans.jpg 2x" data-file-width="606" data-file-height="858" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:GenWmSRosecrans.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
General William S. Rosecrans</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The position of Chief of Staff for a general was usually held by a more junior officer, but Garfield's influence with Rosecrans was greater than usual, with duties extending beyond mere communication of orders to actual management of his <a href="/wiki/Army_of_the_Cumberland" title="Army of the Cumberland">Army of the Cumberland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978176_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978176-57"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup> Rosecrans had a voracious appetite for conversation, especially when he was unable to sleep; in Garfield, he found "the first well read person in the Army" and the ideal candidate for discussions that ran deep into the night.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978169_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978169-58"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup> The two became close, and their talks covered all topics, especially religion; Rosecrans, who had converted from <a href="/wiki/Methodism" title="Methodism">Methodism</a> to <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholicism" title="Roman Catholicism" class="mw-redirect">Roman Catholicism</a>, succeeded in softening Garfield's view of his faith.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978170_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978170-59"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> Garfield recommended that Rosecrans replace wing commanders <a href="/wiki/Alexander_McCook" title="Alexander McCook" class="mw-redirect">Alexander McCook</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Leonidas_Crittenden" title="Thomas Leonidas Crittenden">Thomas Crittenden</a>, whom he believed ineffective; Rosecrans ignored the suggestions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978177_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978177-60"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> With Rosecrans, Garfield devised the <a href="/wiki/Tullahoma_Campaign" title="Tullahoma Campaign">Tullahoma Campaign</a> to pursue and trap Confederate General <a href="/wiki/Braxton_Bragg" title="Braxton Bragg">Braxton Bragg</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tullahoma" title="Tullahoma" class="mw-redirect">Tullahoma</a>. After initial Union success, Bragg retreated toward <a href="/wiki/Chattanooga" title="Chattanooga" class="mw-redirect">Chattanooga</a>, where Rosecrans stalled and requested more troops and supplies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978180.E2.80.93182_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978180.E2.80.93182-61"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> Garfield argued for an immediate advance, in line with demands from Halleck and Lincoln.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978180.E2.80.93182_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978180.E2.80.93182-61"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> After a council of war and lengthy deliberations, Rosecrans agreed to attack.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978183.E2.80.93189_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978183.E2.80.93189-62"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>At the ensuing <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Chickamauga" title="Battle of Chickamauga">Battle of Chickamauga</a> on September 19 and 20, 1863, confusion among the wing commanders over Rosecrans's orders created a gap in the lines, resulting in a rout of the right flank. Rosecrans concluded that the battle was lost and fell back on Chattanooga to establish a defensive line.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978205.E2.80.93208_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978205.E2.80.93208-63"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup> Garfield, however, thought that part of the army had held and, with Rosecrans's approval, headed across <a href="/wiki/Missionary_Ridge" title="Missionary Ridge">Missionary Ridge</a> to survey the scene. Garfield's hunch was correct.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978205.E2.80.93208_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978205.E2.80.93208-63"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup> His ride became legendary, while Rosecrans' error reignited criticism about his leadership.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978205.E2.80.93208_63-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978205.E2.80.93208-63"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup> While Rosecrans's army had avoided disaster, they were stranded in Chattanooga, surrounded by Bragg's army. Garfield sent a telegram to Secretary of War <a href="/wiki/Edwin_M._Stanton" title="Edwin M. Stanton" class="mw-redirect">Edwin M. Stanton</a> alerting Washington to the need for reinforcements to avoid annihilation, and Lincoln and Halleck delivered 20,000 troops by rail within nine days.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978210_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978210-64"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup> In the meantime, Grant was promoted to command of the western armies, and quickly replaced Rosecrans with <a href="/wiki/George_H._Thomas" title="George H. Thomas" class="mw-redirect">George H. Thomas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978213_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978213-65"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup> Garfield was ordered to report to Washington, where he was promoted to major general, a commission he would resign before taking a seat in the House of Representatives.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978219.E2.80.93220_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978219.E2.80.93220-66"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> According to historian <a href="/wiki/Jean_Edward_Smith" title="Jean Edward Smith">Jean Edward Smith</a>, Grant and Garfield had a "guarded relationship", since Grant promoted Thomas to command of the Army of the Cumberland, rather than Garfield, after Rosecrans was dismissed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001550.E2.80.93551_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001550.E2.80.93551-67"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Congressional_career">Congressional career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_A._Garfield&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Congressional career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_in_1862.3B_Civil_War_years">Election in 1862; Civil War years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_A._Garfield&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Election in 1862; Civil War years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mathew_Brady,_Portrait_of_Secretary_of_the_Treasury_Salmon_P._Chase,_officer_of_the_United_States_government_(1860%E2%80%931865).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Mathew_Brady%2C_Portrait_of_Secretary_of_the_Treasury_Salmon_P._Chase%2C_officer_of_the_United_States_government_%281860%E2%80%931865%29.jpg/170px-Mathew_Brady%2C_Portrait_of_Secretary_of_the_Treasury_Salmon_P._Chase%2C_officer_of_the_United_States_government_%281860%E2%80%931865%29.jpg" width="170" height="213" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Mathew_Brady%2C_Portrait_of_Secretary_of_the_Treasury_Salmon_P._Chase%2C_officer_of_the_United_States_government_%281860%E2%80%931865%29.jpg/255px-Mathew_Brady%2C_Portrait_of_Secretary_of_the_Treasury_Salmon_P._Chase%2C_officer_of_the_United_States_government_%281860%E2%80%931865%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Mathew_Brady%2C_Portrait_of_Secretary_of_the_Treasury_Salmon_P._Chase%2C_officer_of_the_United_States_government_%281860%E2%80%931865%29.jpg/340px-Mathew_Brady%2C_Portrait_of_Secretary_of_the_Treasury_Salmon_P._Chase%2C_officer_of_the_United_States_government_%281860%E2%80%931865%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1539" data-file-height="1926" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mathew_Brady,_Portrait_of_Secretary_of_the_Treasury_Salmon_P._Chase,_officer_of_the_United_States_government_(1860%E2%80%931865).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Salmon_P._Chase" title="Salmon P. Chase">Salmon P. Chase</a> was Garfield's ally until Andrew Johnson's impeachment trial</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>While serving in the army in early 1862, Garfield was approached by friends about running for Congress from Ohio's newly redrawn, heavily Republican <a href="/wiki/Ohio%27s_19th_congressional_district" title="Ohio's 19th congressional district">19th district</a>. He was worried that he and other state-appointed generals would get obscure assignments, and running for Congress would allow him to resume his political career. The fact that the new Congress would not hold its first regular session until December 1863<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span>[</span>c<span>]</span></a></sup> would allow him to continue his war service for a time. Home on medical leave, he refused to campaign for the nomination, leaving that to political managers who secured it at the local convention in September 1862, on the eighth ballot. In October, he defeated D.B. Woods by a two-to-one margin in the general election for a seat in the <a href="/wiki/38th_United_States_Congress" title="38th United States Congress">38th Congress</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERutkow200617Peskin1978148_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERutkow200617Peskin1978148-69"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Soon after the nomination, Garfield was ordered to report to War Secretary <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Stanton" title="Edwin Stanton">Edwin Stanton</a> in Washington to discuss his military future. There, Garfield met Treasury Secretary <a href="/wiki/Salmon_P._Chase" title="Salmon P. Chase">Salmon P. Chase</a>, who befriended him, seeing him as a younger version of himself. The two men agreed politically, and both were part of the <a href="/wiki/Radical_Republican" title="Radical Republican">Radical</a> wing of the Republican Party.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERutkow200618_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERutkow200618-70"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> Once he took his seat in December 1863, Garfield was frustrated that Lincoln seemed reluctant to press the South hard. Many radicals, led in the House by Pennsylvania's <a href="/wiki/Thaddeus_Stevens" title="Thaddeus Stevens">Thaddeus Stevens</a>, wanted lands owned by rebels to be confiscated, but Lincoln threatened to veto any bill that would do that on a widespread basis. Garfield, in debate on the House floor, supported such legislation and, discussing England's <a href="/wiki/Glorious_Revolution" title="Glorious Revolution">Glorious Revolution</a>, hinted that Lincoln might be thrown out of office for resisting the bills.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaldwell1965139.E2.80.93142_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaldwell1965139.E2.80.93142-71"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup> Although Garfield had supported Lincoln's <a href="/wiki/Emancipation_Proclamation" title="Emancipation Proclamation">Emancipation Proclamation</a>, the congressman marveled that it was a "strange phenomenon in the world's history, when a second-rate Illinois lawyer is the instrument to utter words which shall form an epoch memorable in all future ages".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERutkow200625.E2.80.9326_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERutkow200625.E2.80.9326-72"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Garfield not only favored abolition, but believed that the leaders of the rebellion had forfeited their constitutional rights. He supported the confiscation of southern plantations and even exile or execution of rebellion leaders as a means to ensure the permanent destruction of slavery.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978233_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978233-73"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> He felt Congress was obliged "to determine what legislation is necessary to secure equal justice to all loyal persons, without regard to color."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978234_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978234-74"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup> Garfield was more supportive when Lincoln took action against slavery.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978152_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978152-75"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup> Early in his tenure, he differed from his party on several issues; his was the solitary Republican vote to terminate the use of bounties in recruiting. Some financially able recruits had used the bounty system to buy their way out of service (called commutation), which Garfield considered reprehensible.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978224_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978224-76"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup> Garfield gave a speech pointing out the flaws in the existing conscription law: that of 300,000 called upon to enlist, barely 10,000 had, the remainder claiming exemption or providing money or a substitute. Lincoln appeared before the Military Affairs committee on which Garfield served, demanding a more effective bill; even if it cost him re-election, Lincoln was confident he could win the war before his term expired.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaldwell1965145.E2.80.93147_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaldwell1965145.E2.80.93147-77"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup> After many false starts, Garfield, with the support of Lincoln, procured the passage of a conscription bill which excluded commutation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978232_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978232-78"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Under Chase's influence, Garfield became a staunch proponent of a dollar backed by a <a href="/wiki/Gold_standard" title="Gold standard">gold standard</a>, and was therefore a strong opponent of the "<a href="/wiki/Greenback_(money)" title="Greenback (money)" class="mw-redirect">greenback</a>"; he regretted very much, but understood, the necessity for <a href="/wiki/Specie_Payment_Resumption_Act" title="Specie Payment Resumption Act">suspension of payment in gold or silver</a> during the emergency presented by the Civil War.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978156_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978156-79"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup> Garfield voted with the Radical Republicans in passing the <a href="/wiki/Wade%E2%80%93Davis_Bill" title="Wade–Davis Bill">Wade–Davis Bill</a>, designed to give Congress more authority over <a href="/wiki/Reconstruction_Era_of_the_United_States" title="Reconstruction Era of the United States" class="mw-redirect">Reconstruction</a>, but it was defeated by Lincoln's <a href="/wiki/Pocket_veto" title="Pocket veto">pocket veto</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978241_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978241-80"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Garfield did not consider Lincoln particularly worthy of re-election, but no viable alternative seemed available. "He will probably be the man, though I think we could do better."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERutkow200625.E2.80.9326_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERutkow200625.E2.80.9326-72"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> The Ohioan attended <a href="/wiki/1864_National_Union_National_Convention" title="1864 National Union National Convention">the party convention</a> and promoted Rosecrans as Lincoln's running mate, but delegates chose Military Governor of Tennessee <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Johnson" title="Andrew Johnson">Andrew Johnson</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978240_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978240-81"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup> Both Lincoln and Garfield were re-elected.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown1881134.E2.80.93137_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown1881134.E2.80.93137-82"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup> By then, Chase had left the Cabinet and had been appointed <a href="/wiki/Chief_Justice_of_the_United_States" title="Chief Justice of the United States">Chief Justice</a>, and his relations with Garfield became more distant.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaldwell1965153_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaldwell1965153-83"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Garfield took up the practice of law in 1865 as a means to improve his personal finances. His efforts took him to Wall Street where, the day after Lincoln's assassination, a riotous crowd led him into an impromptu speech to calm it: "Fellow citizens! Clouds and darkness are round about Him! His pavilion is dark waters and thick clouds of the skies! Justice and judgment are the establishment of His throne! Mercy and truth shall go before His face! Fellow citizens! God reigns, and the Government at Washington still lives!"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978250_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978250-84"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup> The speech, with no mention or praise of Lincoln, was according to Garfield biographer Robert G. Caldwell "quite as significant for what it did not contain as for what it did".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaldwell1965154.E2.80.93155_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaldwell1965154.E2.80.93155-85"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup> In the following years, Garfield had more praise for Lincoln; a year after the Illinoisan's death Garfield stated that "greatest among all these developments were the character and fame of Abraham Lincoln", and in 1878 called Lincoln "one of the few great rulers whose wisdom increased with his power".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaldwell1965155.E2.80.93156_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaldwell1965155.E2.80.93156-86"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reconstruction">Reconstruction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_A._Garfield&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Reconstruction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After the war, Garfield became a proponent of black suffrage, though he admitted that the idea of African Americans as political equals with whites gave him "a strong feeling of repugnance".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoenecke198147.E2.80.9348_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoenecke198147.E2.80.9348-87"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup> The new president, Johnson, sought the rapid restoration of the Southern states during the months between his accession and the meeting of Congress in December 1865; Garfield hesitantly supported this policy as an experiment. Johnson, an old friend, sought Garfield's backing, and their conversations led Garfield to assume that differences between president and Congress were not large. When Congress assembled in December (to Johnson's chagrin without the elected representatives of the Southern states, who were excluded), Garfield urged conciliation on his colleagues, although he feared that Johnson, a former Democrat, might combine with other Democrats to gain political control if he rejoined the party. Garfield foresaw conflict even before February 1866 when Johnson vetoed a bill to extend the life of the <a href="/wiki/Freedmen%27s_Bureau" title="Freedmen's Bureau">Freedmen's Bureau</a>, charged with aiding the former slaves. By April, Garfield had concluded that Johnson was either "crazy or drunk with opium".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978279_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978279-88"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Garfield_Monument1.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Garfield_Monument1.JPG/220px-Garfield_Monument1.JPG" width="220" height="299" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Garfield_Monument1.JPG/330px-Garfield_Monument1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Garfield_Monument1.JPG/440px-Garfield_Monument1.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2297" data-file-height="3124" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Garfield_Monument1.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/James_A._Garfield_Monument" title="James A. Garfield Monument">Garfield Monument</a>, by the Capitol, where he served almost twenty years</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The conflict between the branches of government was the major issue of the 1866 campaign, with Johnson taking to the campaign trail in a <a href="/wiki/Swing_Around_the_Circle" title="Swing Around the Circle">Swing Around the Circle</a> and Garfield facing opposition within his party in his home district. With the South still disenfranchised and Northern public opinion behind them, the Republicans gained a two-thirds majority in both houses of Congress. Garfield, having overcome his challengers at his district nominating convention, was easily re-elected.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaldwell1965170.E2.80.93172_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaldwell1965170.E2.80.93172-89"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Garfield opposed the initial talk of impeaching President Johnson when Congress convened in December 1866.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978278_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978278-90"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup> However, he supported legislation to limit Johnson's powers, such as the <a href="/wiki/Tenure_of_Office_Act_(1867)" title="Tenure of Office Act (1867)">Tenure of Office Act</a>, which restricted Johnson in removing presidential appointees. Distracted by committee duties, he rarely spoke in connection with these bills, but was a loyal Republican vote against Johnson. Due to a court case, he was absent on the day in April 1868 when the House <a href="/wiki/Impeachment_of_Andrew_Johnson" title="Impeachment of Andrew Johnson">impeached Johnson</a>, but soon gave a speech aligning himself with Thaddeus Stevens and others who sought Johnson's removal. When the president was acquitted in trial before the Senate, Garfield was shocked, and blamed the outcome of the trial on its presiding officer, Chief Justice Chase, his onetime mentor.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaldwell1965173.E2.80.93174Peskin1978287.E2.80.93289_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaldwell1965173.E2.80.93174Peskin1978287.E2.80.93289-91"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>By the time Ulysses S. Grant succeeded Johnson in 1869, Garfield had moved away from the remaining radicals (Stevens, their leader, had died in 1868). He hailed the ratification of the <a href="/wiki/Fifteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Fifteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">15th Amendment</a> in 1870 as a triumph, and he favored the re-admission of Georgia to the Union as a matter of right, not politics. In 1871, Garfield opposed passage of the <a href="/wiki/Ku_Klux_Klan_Act" title="Ku Klux Klan Act" class="mw-redirect">Ku Klux Klan Act</a>, saying "I have never been more perplexed by a piece of legislation". He was torn between his indignation at "these terrorists" and his concern for the freedoms endangered by the power the bill gave to the president to enforce the act through suspension of <a href="/wiki/Habeas_corpus" title="Habeas corpus">habeas corpus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978332.E2.80.93334_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978332.E2.80.93334-92"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tariffs_and_finance">Tariffs and finance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_A._Garfield&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Tariffs and finance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:US-$1-LT-1862-Fr-16c.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/US-%241-LT-1862-Fr-16c.jpg/300px-US-%241-LT-1862-Fr-16c.jpg" width="300" height="267" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/US-%241-LT-1862-Fr-16c.jpg/450px-US-%241-LT-1862-Fr-16c.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/US-%241-LT-1862-Fr-16c.jpg/600px-US-%241-LT-1862-Fr-16c.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4500" data-file-height="4001" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:US-$1-LT-1862-Fr-16c.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The greenback so despised by Garfield</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Throughout his political career, Garfield favored the <a href="/wiki/Gold_standard" title="Gold standard">gold standard</a> and decried attempts to increase the money supply through the issuance of paper money not backed by gold, and later, through the <a href="/wiki/Free_silver" title="Free silver">free and unlimited coinage of silver</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaldwell1965205.E2.80.93218_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaldwell1965205.E2.80.93218-93"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1865, Garfield was placed on the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_Committee_on_Ways_and_Means" title="United States House Committee on Ways and Means">House Ways and Means Committee</a>, a long-awaited opportunity to focus on financial and economic issues. He reprised his opposition to the greenback, saying, "any party which commits itself to paper money will go down amid the general disaster, covered with the curses of a ruined people."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978261_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978261-94"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1868 Garfield gave a two-hour speech on currency in the House, which was widely applauded as his best oratory to that point; in it he advocated a gradual resumption of specie payments, that is, the government paying out silver and gold, rather than paper money that could not be redeemed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978268_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978268-95"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Tariffs had been raised to high levels during the Civil War. Afterwards, Garfield, who made a close study of financial affairs, advocated moving towards free trade, though the standard Republican position was a protective tariff that would allow American industries to grow. This break with his party likely cost him his place on the Ways and Means Committee in 1867, and though Republicans held the majority in the House until 1875, Garfield remained off that committee during that time. Garfield came to chair the powerful <a href="/wiki/House_Appropriations_Committee" title="House Appropriations Committee" class="mw-redirect">House Appropriations Committee</a>, but it was Ways and Means, with its influence over fiscal policy, that he really wanted to lead.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERutkow200631.E2.80.9332_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERutkow200631.E2.80.9332-96"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup> Part of the reason Garfield was denied a place on Ways and Means was the opposition of the influential Republican editor, <a href="/wiki/Horace_Greeley" title="Horace Greeley">Horace Greeley</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978265.2C_327_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978265.2C_327-97"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Black_Friday_1869.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A blackboard with columns of numbers. Across the top is a banner that says &quot;Black Friday&quot; and below is a hand written note" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Black_Friday_1869.jpg/220px-Black_Friday_1869.jpg" width="220" height="192" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Black_Friday_1869.jpg/330px-Black_Friday_1869.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Black_Friday_1869.jpg/440px-Black_Friday_1869.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="893" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Black_Friday_1869.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Garfield's handwriting on evidence used during the Gold Panic investigation in 1870</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In September 1870, Garfield, who was then chairman of the <a href="/wiki/House_Banking_Committee" title="House Banking Committee" class="mw-redirect">House Banking Committee</a>, led an investigation into the <a href="/wiki/Black_Friday_(1869)" title="Black Friday (1869)">Black Friday</a> Gold Panic scandal. The committee investigation into corruption was thorough, but found no indictable offenses. Garfield blamed the easy availability of <a href="/wiki/Fiat_money" title="Fiat money">fiat money</a> greenbacks for financing the speculation that led to the scandal.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981328Peskin1978311_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981328Peskin1978311-98"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Garfield was not at all enthused about the re-election of President Grant in 1872—until Horace Greeley, who emerged as the candidate of the Democrats and <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Liberal Republican Party (United States)">Liberal Republicans</a>, became the only serious alternative. Garfield opined, "I would say Grant was not fit to be nominated and Greeley is not fit to be elected."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978350.E2.80.93351_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978350.E2.80.93351-99"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup> Both Grant and Garfield won overwhelming re-election victories.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978350.E2.80.93351_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978350.E2.80.93351-99"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cr.C3.A9dit_Mobilier_scandal.3B_Salary_Grab">Crédit Mobilier scandal; Salary Grab</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_A._Garfield&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Crédit Mobilier scandal; Salary Grab">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Cr%C3%A9dit_Mobilier_of_America_scandal" title="Crédit Mobilier of America scandal">Crédit Mobilier of America scandal</a> involved corruption in the financing of the <a href="/wiki/Union_Pacific_Railroad" title="Union Pacific Railroad">Union Pacific Railroad</a>, part of the <a href="/wiki/Transcontinental_railroad" title="Transcontinental railroad">transcontinental railroad</a> that was completed in 1869. Union Pacific officers and directors secretly purchased control of the <a href="/wiki/Cr%C3%A9dit_Mobilier_of_America" title="Crédit Mobilier of America" class="mw-redirect">Crédit Mobilier of America</a> company, then contracted with the firm to have it undertake the construction of the railroad. The grossly inflated invoices submitted by the company were paid by the railroad, using federal funds appropriated to subsidize the project, and the company was allowed to purchase Union Pacific securities at <a href="/wiki/Par_value" title="Par value">par value</a>, well below the market rate. Crédit Mobilier showed large profits and stock gains, and distributed substantial dividends. The high expenses meant that Congress was called upon to appropriate more funds. One of the railroad officials that controlled Crédit Mobilier was also a congressman, <a href="/wiki/Oakes_Ames" title="Oakes Ames">Oakes Ames</a> of Massachusetts. He offered some of his colleagues the opportunity to buy Crédit Mobilier stock at par value, well below what it sold for on the market, and the railroad got its additional appropriations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaldwell1965219_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaldwell1965219-100"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Keppler_Credit_Mobilier_Hari-Kari.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Keppler_Credit_Mobilier_Hari-Kari.png/275px-Keppler_Credit_Mobilier_Hari-Kari.png" width="275" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Keppler_Credit_Mobilier_Hari-Kari.png/413px-Keppler_Credit_Mobilier_Hari-Kari.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Keppler_Credit_Mobilier_Hari-Kari.png/550px-Keppler_Credit_Mobilier_Hari-Kari.png 2x" data-file-width="2998" data-file-height="1958" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Keppler_Credit_Mobilier_Hari-Kari.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Editorial cartoon: <a href="/wiki/Uncle_Sam" title="Uncle Sam">Uncle Sam</a> directs U.S. Senators and Representatives implicated in the Crédit Mobilier scheme to commit <a href="/wiki/Hara-Kiri" title="Hara-Kiri" class="mw-redirect">Hara-Kiri</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The story broke in July 1872, in the middle of the presidential campaign. Among those named were Vice President (and former House Speaker) <a href="/wiki/Schuyler_Colfax" title="Schuyler Colfax">Schuyler Colfax</a>, Grant's second-term <a href="/wiki/Running_mate" title="Running mate">running mate</a> (Massachusetts Senator <a href="/wiki/Henry_Wilson" title="Henry Wilson">Henry Wilson</a>), Speaker <a href="/wiki/James_G._Blaine" title="James G. Blaine">James G. Blaine</a> of Maine, and Garfield. Greeley had little luck taking advantage of the scandal. When Congress reconvened after the election, Blaine, seeking to clear his name, demanded a House investigation. Evidence before the special committee exonerated Blaine. Garfield had stated, in September 1872, that Ames had offered him stock, but he had repeatedly refused it. Testifying before the committee in January, Ames alleged that he had offered Garfield ten shares of stock at par value, but that Garfield had never taken the shares, or paid for them. A year had passed, from 1867 to 1868, before Garfield had finally refused it. Garfield, appearing before the committee on January 14, 1873, confirmed much of this. Ames testified several weeks later that Garfield agreed to take the stock on credit, and that it was paid for by the company's huge dividends.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaldwell1965224.E2.80.93226_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaldwell1965224.E2.80.93226-101"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup> The two men differed over a sum of some $300 that Garfield received and later paid back, with Garfield deeming it a loan and Ames a dividend.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978354.E2.80.93359_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978354.E2.80.93359-102"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Garfield's biographers were unwilling to exonerate him in Crédit Mobilier, with Allan Peskin writing "Did Garfield lie? Not exactly. Did he tell the truth? Not completely. Was he corrupted? Not really. Even Garfield's enemies never claimed that his involvement&#160;... influenced his behavior".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978362_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978362-103"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup> Rutkow wrote that "Garfield's real offense was that he knowingly denied to the House investigating committee that he had agreed to accept the stock and that he had also received a dividend of $329."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERutkow200634_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERutkow200634-104"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup> Caldwell suggested that Garfield "while he told the truth [before the committee], certainly failed to tell the whole truth, clearly evading an answer to certain vital questions and thus giving the impression of worse faults than those of which he was guilty".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaldwell1965230_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaldwell1965230-105"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup> That Crédit Mobilier was a corrupt organization had been a secret badly kept, even mentioned on the floor of Congress, and editor Sam Bowles wrote at the time that Garfield, in his positions on committees dealing with finance, "had no more right to be ignorant in a matter of such grave importance as this, than the sentinel has to snore on his post".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978362_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978362-103"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Another issue that caused Garfield trouble in his 1874 re-election bid was the so-called "<a href="/wiki/Salary_Grab_Act" title="Salary Grab Act">Salary Grab</a>" of 1873, which increased the compensation for members of Congress by 50 percent, retroactive to 1871. Garfield was responsible, as Appropriations Committee chairman, for shepherding the legislative appropriations bill through the House; during the debate in February 1873, Massachusetts Representative <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Butler_(politician)" title="Benjamin Butler (politician)">Benjamin Butler</a> offered the increase as an amendment, and despite Garfield's opposition, it passed the House and eventually became law. The law was very popular in the House, as almost half the members were <a href="/wiki/Lame_duck_(politics)" title="Lame duck (politics)">lame ducks</a>, but the public was outraged, and many of Garfield's constituents blamed him, though he refused to accept the increase.
