<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Assassination">Assassination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_A._Garfield&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Assassination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Garfield_assassination_engraving_cropped.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Garfield_assassination_engraving_cropped.jpg/220px-Garfield_assassination_engraving_cropped.jpg" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Garfield_assassination_engraving_cropped.jpg/330px-Garfield_assassination_engraving_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Garfield_assassination_engraving_cropped.jpg/440px-Garfield_assassination_engraving_cropped.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1160" data-file-height="790" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Garfield_assassination_engraving_cropped.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Garfield, shot by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Guiteau" title="Charles Guiteau" class="mw-redirect">Charles Guiteau</a>, collapses as Secretary of State Blaine gestures for help. Engraving from <i>The Illustrated Newspaper</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_James_A._Garfield" title="Assassination of James A. Garfield">Assassination of James A. Garfield</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Guiteau_and_shooting">Guiteau and shooting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_A._Garfield&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Guiteau and shooting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Charles_J._Guiteau" title="Charles J. Guiteau">Charles J. Guiteau</a> had followed various professions in his life, but in 1880 had determined to gain federal office by supporting what he expected to be the winning Republican ticket.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003114_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003114-168"><span>[</span>163<span>]</span></a></sup> He composed a speech, "Garfield vs. Hancock", and got it printed by the Republican National Committee. One means of persuading the voter in that era was through orators expounding on the candidate's merits, but with the Republicans seeking more famous men, Guiteau received few opportunities to speak.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003151.E2.80.93152_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003151.E2.80.93152-169"><span>[</span>164<span>]</span></a></sup> On one occasion, according to Kenneth D. Ackerman in his book about Garfield's candidacy and assassination, Guiteau was unable to finish his speech due to nerves. Guiteau, who considered himself a Stalwart, deemed his contribution to Garfield's victory sufficient to justify the position of consul in Paris, despite the fact he spoke no French, nor any foreign language.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003189.E2.80.93190.2C_237_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003189.E2.80.93190.2C_237-170"><span>[</span>165<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>One of President Garfield's more wearying duties was seeing office seekers, and he saw Guiteau at least once. White House officials suggested that Guiteau approach Blaine, as the consulship was within the Department of State.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003237.E2.80.93238_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003237.E2.80.93238-171"><span>[</span>166<span>]</span></a></sup> Blaine also saw the public regularly, and Guiteau became a regular at these sessions; Blaine, who had no intention of giving Guiteau a position he was not qualified for and had not earned, simply stated that the deadlock in the Senate over Robertson's nomination made it impossible to consider the Paris consulship, which required Senate confirmation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003278.E2.80.93279_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003278.E2.80.93279-172"><span>[</span>167<span>]</span></a></sup> Once the New York senators had resigned, and Robertson had been confirmed as Collector, Guiteau pressed his claim, and Blaine told him he would not receive the position.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003299_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003299-173"><span>[</span>168<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Guiteau came to believe he had lost the position because he was a Stalwart. The office-seeker decided that the only way to end the internecine warfare in the Republican Party was for Garfield to die—though he had nothing personal against the president. Arthur's succession would restore peace, he felt, and lead to rewards for fellow Stalwarts, including Guiteau.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003305.E2.80.93308_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003305.E2.80.93308-174"><span>[</span>169<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Abraham_Lincoln" title="Assassination of Abraham Lincoln">assassination of Abraham Lincoln</a> was deemed a fluke due to the Civil War, and Garfield, like most people, saw no reason why the president should be guarded; Garfield's movements and plans were often printed in the newspapers. Guiteau knew the president would leave Washington for cooler climes on July 2, and made plans to kill him before then. He purchased a gun he thought would look good in a museum, and followed Garfield several times, but each time his plans were frustrated, or he lost his nerve.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003313.E2.80.93316_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003313.E2.80.93316-175"><span>[</span>170<span>]</span></a></sup> His opportunities dwindled to one—Garfield's departure by train for Massachusetts on the morning of July 2, 1881.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003328.E2.80.93330_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003328.E2.80.93330-176"><span>[</span>171<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Guiteau concealed himself by the ladies' waiting room at the <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_and_Potomac_Railroad_Station" title="Baltimore and Potomac Railroad Station">Sixth Street Station</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_and_Potomac_Railroad" title="Baltimore and Potomac Railroad">Baltimore and Potomac Railroad</a>, from where Garfield was scheduled to depart. Most of Garfield's cabinet planned to accompany him at least part of the way; Blaine, who was to remain in Washington, came to the station to see him off. The two men were deep in conversation and did not notice Guiteau before he took out his revolver and shot Garfield twice, once in the back and once in the arm. The time was 9:30 am. The assassin attempted to leave the station, but was quickly captured.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978596_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978596-177"><span>[</span>172<span>]</span></a></sup> As Blaine recognized him and Guiteau made no secret of why he had shot Garfield, the assassin's motivation to benefit the Stalwarts reached many with the early news of the shooting, causing rage against that faction.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003335.E2.80.93340_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003335.E2.80.93340-178"><span>[</span>173<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Treatment_and_death">Treatment and death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_A._Garfield&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Treatment and death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:B%26PStation1908.jpg" class="image"><img alt="An ornate Victorian Gothic style building with a square tower" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/B%26PStation1908.jpg/220px-B%26PStation1908.jpg" width="220" height="269" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/B%26PStation1908.jpg/330px-B%26PStation1908.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/B%26PStation1908.jpg/440px-B%26PStation1908.jpg 2x" data-file-width="480" data-file-height="586" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:B%26PStation1908.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Baltimore_%26_Potomac_Railroad" title="Baltimore &amp; Potomac Railroad" class="mw-redirect">Baltimore &amp; Potomac Railroad</a> Passenger Terminal in Washington where Garfield was shot July 2, 1881.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Garfield was hit by two shots; one glanced off his arm while the other pierced his back, shattering a rib and embedding itself in his abdomen. "My God, what is this?" he exclaimed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003333.E2.80.93334_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003333.E2.80.93334-179"><span>[</span>174<span>]</span></a></sup> Guiteau, as he was led away, stated, "I did it. I will go to jail for it. I am a Stalwart and Arthur will be President."<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180"><span>[</span>f<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003335_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003335-181"><span>[</span>175<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Among those at the station was <a href="/wiki/Robert_Todd_Lincoln" title="Robert Todd Lincoln">Robert Todd Lincoln</a>, who sixteen years before had watched <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Abraham_Lincoln" title="Assassination of Abraham Lincoln">his father die</a> from an assassin's bullet. Garfield was taken on a mattress upstairs to a private office, where several doctors examined him, probing the wound with unwashed fingers. At his request, Garfield was taken back to the White House, and his wife, then in New Jersey, was sent for.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003335.E2.80.93336_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003335.E2.80.93336-182"><span>[</span>176<span>]</span></a></sup> Blaine sent word to Vice President Arthur in New York City, who received threats against his life because of his animosity toward Garfield and Guiteau's statements.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERutkow200688.E2.80.9389_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERutkow200688.E2.80.9389-183"><span>[</span>177<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Although <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Lister,_1st_Baron_Lister" title="Joseph Lister, 1st Baron Lister">Joseph Lister</a>'s pioneering work in antisepsis was known to American doctors (Lister himself had visited America in 1876), few of them had confidence in it, and none of his advocates were among Garfield's treating physicians.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERutkow2006106.E2.80.93107_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERutkow2006106.E2.80.93107-184"><span>[</span>178<span>]</span></a></sup> The physician who took charge at the depot and then at the White House was <a href="/wiki/Doctor_Willard_Bliss" title="Doctor Willard Bliss">Doctor Willard Bliss</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185"><span>[</span>g<span>]</span></a></sup> A noted physician and surgeon, Bliss was an old friend of Garfield, and about a dozen doctors, led by Bliss, were soon probing the wound with unsterilized fingers and instruments. Garfield was given morphine for the pain, and asked Bliss to frankly tell him his chances, which Bliss put at one in a hundred. "Well, Doctor, we'll take that chance."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003346_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003346-186"><span>[</span>179<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Over the next few days, Garfield made some improvement, as the nation viewed the news from the capital and prayed. Although he never stood again, he was able to sit up and write several times, and his recovery was viewed so positively that a steamer was fitted out as a seagoing hospital to aid with his convalescence. He was nourished on oatmeal (which he detested) and milk from a cow on the White House lawn. When told that Indian chief <a href="/wiki/Sitting_Bull" title="Sitting Bull">Sitting Bull</a>, a prisoner of the army, was starving, Garfield said, "Let him starve", then, "Oh no, send him my oatmeal."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978601.E2.80.93602_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978601.E2.80.93602-187"><span>[</span>180<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Graham_Bell" title="Alexander Graham Bell">Alexander Graham Bell</a> tried to locate the bullet with a primitive metal detector; he was not successful. One means of keeping the president comfortable in Washington's summer heat was one of the first successful <a href="/wiki/Air_conditioning" title="Air conditioning">air conditioning</a> units: air propelled by fans over ice and then dried reduced the temperature in the sickroom by 20 degrees Fahrenheit (11 degrees Celsius).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978601.E2.80.93602_187-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978601.E2.80.93602-187"><span>[</span>180<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Beginning on July 23, Garfield took a turn for the worse. His temperature increased to 104&#160;°F (40&#160;°C); doctors, concerned by a <a href="/wiki/Pus" title="Pus">pus</a> sac that had developed by the wound, operated and inserted a drainage tube. This initially seemed to help, and Garfield, in his bed, was able to hold a brief cabinet meeting on July 29, though members were under orders from Bliss to discuss nothing that might excite Garfield.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978603_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978603-188"><span>[</span>181<span>]</span></a></sup> Doctors probed the sac, which went into Garfield's body, hoping to find the bullet; they most likely only made the infections worse. Garfield performed only one state act in August, signing an extradition paper. By the end of the month, the president was much more feeble than he had been, and his weight had decreased to 130 pounds (59&#160;kg).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003374.E2.80.93375_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003374.E2.80.93375-189"><span>[</span>182<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Garfield had long been anxious to escape hot, unhealthy Washington, and in early September the doctors agreed to move him to Elberon, where his wife had recovered earlier in the summer. He left the White House for the last time on September 5, traveling in a specially cushioned railway car; a spur line to the Franklyn Cottage, a seaside mansion given over to his use, was built in a night by volunteers. There, Garfield could see the ocean as officials and reporters maintained what became (after an initial rally) a death watch. Garfield's personal secretary, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Joe_Stanley_Brown&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Joe Stanley Brown (page does not exist)">Joe Stanley Brown</a>, wrote 40 years later, "to this day I cannot hear the sound of the low slow roll of the Atlantic on the shore, the sound which filled my ears as I walked from my cottage to his bedside, without recalling again that ghastly tragedy."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003374.E2.80.93376_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003374.E2.80.93376-190"><span>[</span>183<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On September 18, Garfield asked A.&#160;F. Rockwell, a friend, if he would have a place in history. Rockwell assured him he would, and told Garfield he had much work still before him. "No, my work is done."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaldwell1965355_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaldwell1965355-191"><span>[</span>184<span>]</span></a></sup> The following day, Garfield, by then also suffering from pneumonia and heart pains, marveled that he could not pick up a glass despite feeling well, and went to sleep without discomfort. He awoke that evening around 10:15 pm with great pain in his chest. The attendant watching him sent for Bliss, who found him unconscious. Despite efforts to revive him, Garfield never awoke, dying at 10:35 pm that evening.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003376.E2.80.93377Rutkow2006127_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003376.E2.80.93377Rutkow2006127-192"><span>[</span>185<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>According to some historians and medical experts Garfield might have survived his wounds, had the doctors attending him had at their disposal today's medical research, techniques, and equipment.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchaffer2006_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaffer2006-193"><span>[</span>186<span>]</span></a></sup> Standard medical practice at the time dictated that priority be given to locating the path of the bullet. Several of his doctors inserted their <a href="/wiki/Asepsis" title="Asepsis">unsterilized</a> fingers into the wound to probe for the bullet, a common practice in the 1880s.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchaffer2006_193-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaffer2006-193"><span>[</span>186<span>]</span></a></sup> Historians agree that massive infection was a significant factor in President Garfield's demise.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchaffer2006_193-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaffer2006-193"><span>[</span>186<span>]</span></a></sup> Biographer Peskin stated that medical malpractice did not contribute to Garfield's death; the inevitable infection and blood poisoning that would ensue from a deep bullet wound resulted in damage to multiple organs and spinal bone fragmentation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978607_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978607-194"><span>[</span>187<span>]</span></a></sup> Rutkow, a professor of surgery at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Medicine_and_Dentistry_of_New_Jersey" title="University of Medicine and Dentistry of New Jersey">University of Medicine and Dentistry of New Jersey</a>, has argued that starvation also played a role. Rutkow suggests that "Garfield had such a nonlethal wound. In today's world, he would have gone home in a matter of two or three days."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchaffer2006_193-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaffer2006-193"><span>[</span>186<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Guiteau was indicted on October 14, 1881, for the murder of the president. In a chaotic trial in which Guiteau often interrupted and argued, and in which his counsel used the <a href="/wiki/Insanity_defense" title="Insanity defense">insanity defense</a>, the jury found him guilty on January 5, 1882, and he was sentenced to death. Guiteau may have had <a href="/wiki/Syphilis" title="Syphilis">syphilis</a>, a disease that causes physiological mental impairment.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195"><span>[</span>188<span>]</span></a></sup> He was executed on June 30, 1882.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003444.E2.80.93446_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003444.E2.80.93446-196"><span>[</span>189<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Funeral.2C_memorials_and_commemorations">Funeral, memorials and commemorations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_A._Garfield&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Funeral, memorials and commemorations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Garfield-casket.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Garfield-casket.jpg/220px-Garfield-casket.jpg" width="220" height="163" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Garfield-casket.jpg/330px-Garfield-casket.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Garfield-casket.jpg/440px-Garfield-casket.jpg 2x" data-file-width="928" data-file-height="686" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Garfield's casket <a href="/wiki/Lying_in_state" title="Lying in state">lying in state</a> at the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Capitol_rotunda" title="United States Capitol rotunda">Capitol Rotunda</a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:GarfieldMonument.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/GarfieldMonument.jpg/220px-GarfieldMonument.jpg" width="220" height="348" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/GarfieldMonument.jpg/330px-GarfieldMonument.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/GarfieldMonument.jpg/440px-GarfieldMonument.jpg 2x" data-file-width="762" data-file-height="1204" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/James_A._Garfield_Memorial" title="James A. Garfield Memorial">Garfield Memorial</a> at <a href="/wiki/Lake_View_Cemetery_(Cleveland)" title="Lake View Cemetery (Cleveland)" class="mw-redirect">Lake View Cemetery</a> in Cleveland, Ohio</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Abram_Garfield_Monument,_San_Francisco.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/James_Abram_Garfield_Monument%2C_San_Francisco.jpg/220px-James_Abram_Garfield_Monument%2C_San_Francisco.jpg" width="220" height="330" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/James_Abram_Garfield_Monument%2C_San_Francisco.jpg/330px-James_Abram_Garfield_Monument%2C_San_Francisco.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/James_Abram_Garfield_Monument%2C_San_Francisco.jpg/440px-James_Abram_Garfield_Monument%2C_San_Francisco.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1735" data-file-height="2600" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Garfield Monument, <a href="/wiki/Golden_Gate_Park" title="Golden Gate Park">Golden Gate Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">San Francisco</a> by <a href="/wiki/Frank_Happersberger" title="Frank Happersberger">Frank Happersberger</a></div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Garfield's funeral train left Long Branch on the same special track that brought him there, traveling over tracks blanketed with flowers and past houses adorned with flags. His body was transported to the Capitol and then continued on to Cleveland for burial.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978608.E2.80.93609_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978608.E2.80.93609-197"><span>[</span>190<span>]</span></a></sup> More than 70,000 citizens, some waiting over three hours, passed by Garfield's coffin as his body lay in state in Washington; later, on September 25, 1881, in Cleveland, more than 150,000—a number equal to the entire population of that city—likewise paid their respects.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978608.E2.80.93609_197-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978608.E2.80.93609-197"><span>[</span>190<span>]</span></a></sup> His body was temporarily interred in a vault in Cleveland's <a href="/wiki/Lake_View_Cemetery" title="Lake View Cemetery">Lake View Cemetery</a> until his permanent memorial was built.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978608.E2.80.93609_197-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978608.E2.80.93609-197"><span>[</span>190<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Memorials to Garfield were erected across the country. On April 10, 1882, seven months after Garfield's death, the U.S. Post Office issued a postage stamp in his honor, the second stamp issued by the U.S. to honor an assassinated president.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmithsonian_2006_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmithsonian_2006-198"><span>[</span>191<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1884, sculptor <a href="/wiki/Frank_Happersberger" title="Frank Happersberger">Frank Happersberger</a> completed a monument on the grounds of the <a href="/wiki/Conservatory_of_Flowers" title="Conservatory of Flowers">San Francisco Conservatory of Flowers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERadfordRadford200223_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERadfordRadford200223-199"><span>[</span>192<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1887, the <a href="/wiki/James_A._Garfield_Monument" title="James A. Garfield Monument">James A. Garfield Monument</a> was dedicated in Washington.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003441_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003441-200"><span>[</span>193<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/James_Garfield_Memorial,_Philadelphia" title="James Garfield Memorial, Philadelphia">Another monument</a>, in Philadelphia's <a href="/wiki/Fairmount_Park" title="Fairmount Park">Fairmount Park</a>, was erected in 1896.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBach1992207_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBach1992207-201"><span>[</span>194<span>]</span></a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Victoria,_Australia" title="Victoria, Australia" class="mw-redirect">Victoria, Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Garfield,_Victoria" title="Garfield, Victoria">Cannibal Creek</a> was renamed Garfield in his honor.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202"><span>[</span>195<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On May 19, 1890, Garfield's body was permanently interred, with great solemnity and fanfare, in a <a href="/wiki/Mausoleum" title="Mausoleum">mausoleum</a> in Lake View Cemetery in Cleveland. Attending the dedication ceremonies were former president Hayes, President Benjamin Harrison, and future president <a href="/wiki/William_McKinley" title="William McKinley">William McKinley</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMemorial_189046.E2.80.9349_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMemorial_189046.E2.80.9349-203"><span>[</span>196<span>]</span></a></sup> Garfield's Treasury Secretary, William Windom, also attended.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMemorial_189046.E2.80.9349_203-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMemorial_189046.E2.80.9349-203"><span>[</span>196<span>]</span></a></sup> Harrison said that Garfield was always a "student and instructor" and that his life works and death would "continue to be instructive and inspiring incidents in American history".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMemorial_189051_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMemorial_189051-204"><span>[</span>197<span>]</span></a></sup> Three panels on the monument display Garfield as a teacher, Union major general, and <a href="/wiki/Orator" title="Orator">orator</a>; another shows him taking the Presidential oath, and a fifth shows his body lying in state at the Capitol rotunda in Washington D.C.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMemorial_189034.E2.80.9335_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMemorial_189034.E2.80.9335-205"><span>[</span>198<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Garfield's murder by a deranged office seeker awakened public awareness of the need for civil service reform legislation. Senator <a href="/wiki/George_H._Pendleton" title="George H. Pendleton">George H. Pendleton</a>, a Democrat from Ohio, launched a reform effort that resulted in the <a href="/wiki/Pendleton_Act" title="Pendleton Act" class="mw-redirect">Pendleton Act</a> in January 1883.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoenecke1981100.E2.80.93102_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoenecke1981100.E2.80.93102-206"><span>[</span>199<span>]</span></a></sup> This act reversed the "spoils system" where office seekers paid up or gave political service in order to obtain or keep federally appointed positions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoenecke1981100.E2.80.93102_206-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoenecke1981100.E2.80.93102-206"><span>[</span>199<span>]</span></a></sup> Under the act, appointments were awarded on merit and competitive examination.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoenecke1981102.E2.80.93103_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoenecke1981102.E2.80.93103-207"><span>[</span>200<span>]</span></a></sup> To ensure the reform was implemented, Congress and Arthur established and funded the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Service_Commission" title="Civil Service Commission" class="mw-redirect">Civil Service Commission</a>. The Pendleton Act, however, only covered 10% of federal government workers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoenecke1981102.E2.80.93103_207-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoenecke1981102.E2.80.93103-207"><span>[</span>200<span>]</span></a></sup> For Arthur, previously known for having been a "veteran spoilsman", civil service reform became his most noteworthy achievement.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978610_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978610-208"><span>[</span>201<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy_and_historical_view">Legacy and historical view</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_A._Garfield&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Legacy and historical view">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>For a few years after his assassination, Garfield's life story was seen as an exemplar of the American success story: that even the poorest boy might someday become President of the United States. Peskin noted that "in mourning Garfield, Americans were not only honoring a president; they were paying tribute to a man whose life story embodied their own most cherished aspirations".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin2000_209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin2000-209"><span>[</span>202<span>]</span></a></sup> As the rivalry between Stalwarts and Half-Breeds faded from the scene in the late 1880s and after, so too did memories of Garfield. In the 1890s, Americans became disillusioned with politicians, and looked elsewhere for inspiration, focusing on industrialists, labor leaders, scientists, and others as their heroes. Increasingly, Garfield's short time as president was forgotten.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERutkow2006136_210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERutkow2006136-210"><span>[</span>203<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The 20th century saw no revival for Garfield. <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Wolfe" title="Thomas Wolfe">Thomas Wolfe</a> deemed the presidents of the <a href="/wiki/Gilded_Age" title="Gilded Age">Gilded Age</a>, including Garfield, "lost Americans" whose "gravely vacant and bewhiskered faces mixed, melted, swam together".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003399_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003399-211"><span>[</span>204<span>]</span></a></sup> The politicians of the Gilded Age faded from the public eye, their luster eclipsed by those who had influenced America outside of political office during that time: the <a href="/wiki/Robber_baron" title="Robber baron">robber barons</a>, the inventors, those who had sought social reform, and others who had lived as America rapidly changed. Current events and more recent figures occupied America's attention: according to Ackerman, "the busy Twentieth Century has made Garfield's era seem remote and irrelevant, its leaders ridiculed for their very obscurity."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003399_211-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003399-211"><span>[</span>204<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Garfield's biographers, and those who have studied his presidency, tend to think well of him, and that his presidency saw a promising start before its untimely end. Historian Justus D. Doenecke, while deeming Garfield a bit of an enigma, chronicles his achievements, "by winning a victory over the Stalwarts, he enhanced both the power and prestige of his office. As a man, he was intelligent, sensitive, and alert, and his knowledge of how government worked was unmatched."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoenecke1981182_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoenecke1981182-212"><span>[</span>205<span>]</span></a></sup> Yet Doenecke criticizes Garfield's dismissal of Merritt in Robertson's favor, and wonders if the president was truly in command of the situation even after the latter's confirmation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoenecke1981183_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoenecke1981183-213"><span>[</span>206<span>]</span></a></sup> According to Caldwell, writing in 1931, "If Garfield lives in history, it will be partly on account of the charm of his personality—but also because in life and in death, he struck the first shrewd blows against a dangerous system of boss rule which seemed for a time about to engulf the politics of the nation. Perhaps if he had lived he could have done no more."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaldwell1965363_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaldwell1965363-214"><span>[</span>207<span>]</span></a></sup> Rutkow writes, "James Abram Garfield's presidency is reduced to a tantalizing 'what if.<span style="padding-right:.2em;">'</span>"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERutkow2006136_210-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERutkow2006136-210"><span>[</span>203<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Peskin believes Garfield deserves more credit for his political career than he has received:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>True, his accomplishments were neither bold nor heroic, but his was not an age that called for heroism. His stormy presidency was brief and, and in some respects, unfortunate, but he did leave the office stronger than he found it. As a public man he had a hand in almost every issue of national importance for almost two decades, while as a party leader he, along with Blaine, forged the Republican Party into the instrument that would lead the United States into the twentieth century.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978611_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978611-215"><span>[</span>208<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_A._Garfield&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 24em; -webkit-column-width: 24em; column-width: 24em; list-style-type: decimal;">
